<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Futures and promises</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
								<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Promise_theory" title="Promise theory">Promise theory</a>.</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, <b>future</b>, <b>promise</b>, <b>delay</b>, and <b>deferred</b> refer to constructs used for <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_(computer_science)" title="Synchronization (computer science)">synchronizing</a> program <a href="/wiki/Execution_(computing)" title="Execution (computing)">execution</a> in some <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent programming language">concurrent programming languages</a>. They describe an object that acts as a proxy for a result that is initially unknown, usually because the <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">computation</a> of its value is yet incomplete.</p>
<p>The term <i>promise</i> was proposed in 1976 by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_P._Friedman" title="Daniel P. Friedman">Daniel P. Friedman</a> and David Wise,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and Peter Hibbard called it <i>eventual</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> A somewhat similar concept <i>future</i> was introduced in 1977 in a paper by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Baker_(computer_scientist)" title="Henry Baker (computer scientist)">Henry Baker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Hewitt" title="Carl Hewitt">Carl Hewitt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The terms <i>future</i>, <i>promise</i>, <i>delay</i>, and <i>deferred</i> are often used interchangeably, although some differences in usage between <i>future</i> and <i>promise</i> are treated below. Specifically, when usage is distinguished, a future is a <i>read-only</i> placeholder view of a variable, while a promise is a writable, <a href="/wiki/Single_assignment" class="mw-redirect" title="Single assignment">single assignment</a> container which sets the value of the future.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Notably, a future may be defined without specifying which specific promise will set its value, and different possible promises may set the value of a given future, though this can be done only once for a given future. In other cases a future and a promise are created together and associated with each other: the future is the value, the promise is the function that sets the value – essentially the return value (future) of an asynchronous function (promise). Setting the value of a future is also called <i>resolving</i>, <i>fulfilling</i>, or <i>binding</i> it.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Futures and promises originated in <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional programming</a> and related paradigms (such as <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic programming</a>) to decouple a value (a future) from how it was computed (a promise), allowing the computation to be done more flexibly, notably by parallelizing it. Later, it found use in <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>, in reducing the latency from communication round trips. Later still, it gained more use by allowing writing asynchronous programs in <a href="/wiki/Direct_style" title="Direct style">direct style</a>, rather than in <a href="/wiki/Continuation-passing_style" title="Continuation-passing style">continuation-passing style</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Implicit_vs._explicit">Implicit vs. explicit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Implicit vs. explicit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Use of futures may be <i>implicit</i> (any use of the future automatically obtains its value, as if it were an ordinary <a href="/wiki/Reference_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reference (programming)">reference</a>) or <i>explicit</i> (the user must call a function to obtain the value, such as the <code>get</code> method of <code><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/10/docs/api/java/util/concurrent/Future.html">java.util.concurrent.Future</a></code> or <code><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/10/docs/api/java/util/concurrent/CompletableFuture.html">java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture</a></code> in <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>). Obtaining the value of an explicit future can be called <i>stinging</i> or <i>forcing</i>. Explicit futures can be implemented as a library, whereas implicit futures are usually implemented as part of the language.</p>
<p>The original Baker and Hewitt paper described implicit futures, which are naturally supported in the <a href="/wiki/Actor_model" title="Actor model">actor model</a> of computation and pure <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a> languages like <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a>. The Friedman and Wise paper described only explicit futures, probably reflecting the difficulty of efficiently implementing implicit futures on stock hardware. The difficulty is that stock hardware does not deal with futures for primitive data types like integers. For example, an add instruction does not know how to deal with <code>3 + <i>future</i> factorial(100000)</code>. In pure actor or object languages this problem can be solved by sending <code><i>future</i> factorial(100000)</code> the message <code>+[3]</code>, which asks the future to add <code>3</code> to itself and return the result. Note that the message passing approach works regardless of when <code>factorial(100000)</code> finishes computation and that no stinging/forcing is needed.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Promise_pipelining">Promise pipelining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Promise pipelining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The use of futures can dramatically reduce <a href="/wiki/Latency_(engineering)" title="Latency (engineering)">latency</a> in <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed systems</a>. For instance, futures enable <i>promise pipelining</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> as implemented in the languages <a href="/wiki/E_(programming_language)" title="E (programming language)">E</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joule_(programming_language)" title="Joule (programming language)">Joule</a>, which was also called <i>call-stream</i><sup id="cite_ref-SIGPLAN_pp._260_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SIGPLAN_pp._260-7">[7]</a></sup> in the language <a href="/wiki/Argus_(programming_language)" title="Argus (programming language)">Argus</a>.</p>
<p>Consider an expression involving conventional <a href="/wiki/Remote_procedure_call" title="Remote procedure call">remote procedure calls</a>, such as:</p>
<pre>
 t3 := ( x.a() ).c( y.b() )
</pre>
<p>which could be expanded to</p>
<pre>
 t1 := x.a();
 t2 := y.b();
 t3 := t1.c(t2);
</pre>
<p>Each statement needs a message to be sent and a reply received before the next statement can proceed. Suppose, for example, that <code>x</code>, <code>y</code>, <code>t1</code>, and <code>t2</code> are all located on the same remote machine. In this case, two complete network round-trips to that machine must take place before the third statement can begin to execute. The third statement will then cause yet another round-trip to the same remote machine.</p>
<p>Using futures, the above expression could be written</p>
<pre>
 t3 := (x &lt;- a()) &lt;- c(y &lt;- b())
</pre>
<p>which could be expanded to</p>
<pre>
 t1 := x &lt;- a();
 t2 := y &lt;- b();
 t3 := t1 &lt;- c(t2);
</pre>
<p>The syntax used here is that of the language E, where <code>x &lt;- a()</code> means to send the message <code>a()</code> asynchronously to <code>x</code>. All three variables are immediately assigned futures for their results, and execution proceeds to subsequent statements. Later attempts to resolve the value of <code>t3</code> may cause a delay; however, pipelining can reduce the number of round-trips needed. If, as in the prior example, <code>x</code>, <code>y</code>, <code>t1</code>, and <code>t2</code> are all located on the same remote machine, a pipelined implementation can compute <code>t3</code> with one round-trip instead of three. Because all three messages are destined for objects which are on the same remote machine, only one request need be sent and only one response need be received containing the result. Note also that the send <code>t1 &lt;- c(t2)</code> would not block even if <code>t1</code> and <code>t2</code> were on different machines to each other, or to <code>x</code> or <code>y</code>.</p>
<p>Promise pipelining should be distinguished from parallel asynchronous message passing. In a system supporting parallel message passing but not pipelining, the message sends <code>x &lt;- a()</code> and <code>y &lt;- b()</code> in the above example could proceed in parallel, but the send of <code>t1 &lt;- c(t2)</code> would have to wait until both <code>t1</code> and <code>t2</code> had been received, even when <code>x</code>, <code>y</code>, <code>t1</code>, and <code>t2</code> are on the same remote machine. The relative latency advantage of pipelining becomes even greater in more complicated situations involving many messages.</p>
<p>Promise pipelining also should not be confused with <a href="/wiki/Actor_model#No_requirement_on_order_of_message_arrival" title="Actor model">pipelined message processing</a> in actor systems, where it is possible for an actor to specify and begin executing a behaviour for the next message before having completed processing of the current message.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Read-only_views">Read-only views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Read-only views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In some programming languages such as <a href="/wiki/Oz_(programming_language)" title="Oz (programming language)">Oz</a>, <a href="/wiki/E_(programming_language)" title="E (programming language)">E</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AmbientTalk" title="AmbientTalk">AmbientTalk</a>, it is possible to obtain a <i>read-only view</i> of a future, which allows reading its value when resolved, but does not permit resolving it:</p>
<ul>
<li>In Oz, the <code>!!</code> operator is used to obtain a read-only view.</li>
<li>In E and AmbientTalk, a future is represented by a pair of values called a <i>promise/resolver pair</i>. The promise represents the read-only view, and the resolver is needed to set the future's value.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B11" title="C++11">C++11</a> a <code>std::future</code> provides a read-only view. The value is set directly by using a <code>std::promise</code>, or set to the result of a function call using <code>std::packaged_task</code> or <code>std::async</code>.</li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/Dojo_Toolkit" title="Dojo Toolkit">Dojo Toolkit</a>'s Deferred API as of version 1.5, a <i>consumer-only promise object</i> represents a read-only view.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Alice_ML" class="mw-redirect" title="Alice ML">Alice ML</a>, futures provide a <i>read-only view</i>, whereas a promise contains both a future and the ability to resolve the future<sup id="cite_ref-Alice_ML_promise_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alice_ML_promise-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alice_ML_future_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alice_ML_future-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/.NET_Framework_4.0" class="mw-redirect" title=".NET Framework 4.0">.NET 4.0</a> <code>System.Threading.Tasks.Task&lt;T&gt;</code> represents a read-only view. Resolving the value can be done via <code>System.Threading.Tasks.TaskCompletionSource&lt;T&gt;</code>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Support for read-only views is consistent with the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_least_privilege" title="Principle of least privilege">principle of least privilege</a>, since it enables the ability to set the value to be restricted to <a href="/wiki/Subject_(access_control)" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject (access control)">subjects</a> that need to set it. In a system that also supports pipelining, the sender of an asynchronous message (with result) receives the read-only promise for the result, and the target of the message receives the resolver.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thread-specific_futures">Thread-specific futures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Thread-specific futures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some languages, such as <a href="/wiki/Alice_(programming_language)" title="Alice (programming language)">Alice ML</a>, define futures that are associated with a specific thread that computes the future's value.<sup id="cite_ref-Alice_ML_future_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alice_ML_future-10">[10]</a></sup> This computation can start either <a href="/wiki/Eager_evaluation" title="Eager evaluation">eagerly</a> when the future is created, or <a href="/wiki/Lazy_evaluation" title="Lazy evaluation">lazily</a> when its value is first needed. A lazy future is similar to a <a href="/wiki/Thunk_(functional_programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thunk (functional programming)">thunk</a>, in the sense of a delayed computation.</p>
<p>Alice ML also supports futures that can be resolved by any thread, and calls these <i>promises</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alice_ML_promise_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alice_ML_promise-9">[9]</a></sup> This use of <i>promise</i> is different from its use in E as described <a href="#Read-only_views">above</a>. In Alice, a promise is not a read-only view, and promise pipelining is unsupported. Instead, pipelining naturally happens for futures, including ones associated with promises.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Blocking_vs_non-blocking_semantics">Blocking vs non-blocking semantics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Blocking vs non-blocking semantics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>If the value of a future is accessed asynchronously, for example by sending a message to it, or by explicitly waiting for it using a construct such as <code>when</code> in E, then there is no difficulty in delaying until the future is resolved before the message can be received or the wait completes. This is the only case to be considered in purely asynchronous systems such as pure actor languages.</p>
<p>However, in some systems it may also be possible to attempt to <i>immediately</i> or <i>synchronously</i> access a future's value. Then there is a design choice to be made:</p>
<ul>
<li>the access could block the current thread or process until the future is resolved (possibly with a timeout). This is the semantics of <i>dataflow variables</i> in the language <a href="/wiki/Oz_(programming_language)" title="Oz (programming language)">Oz</a>.</li>
<li>the attempted synchronous access could always signal an error, for example throwing an <a href="/wiki/Exception_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Exception (computer science)">exception</a>. This is the semantics of remote promises in E.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li>potentially, the access could succeed if the future is already resolved, but signal an error if it is not. This would have the disadvantage of introducing nondeterminism and the potential for <a href="/wiki/Race_conditions" class="mw-redirect" title="Race conditions">race conditions</a>, and seems to be an uncommon design choice.</li>
</ul>
<p>As an example of the first possibility, in <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B11" title="C++11">C++11</a>, a thread that needs the value of a future can block until it is available by calling the <code>wait()</code> or <code>get()</code> member functions. You can also specify a timeout on the wait using the <code>wait_for()</code> or <code>wait_until()</code> member functions to avoid indefinite blocking. If the future arose from a call to <code>std::async</code> then a blocking wait (without a timeout) may cause synchronous invocation of the function to compute the result on the waiting thread.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_constructs">Related constructs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Related constructs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Future</i> is a particular case of <a href="/wiki/Event_(synchronization_primitive)" title="Event (synchronization primitive)">Event (synchronization primitive)</a>, which can be completed only once. In general, events can be reset to initial empty state and, thus, completed as many times as you like.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <i>I-var</i> (as in the language <a href="/wiki/Id_(programming_language)" title="Id (programming language)">Id</a>) is a future with blocking semantics as defined above. An <i>I-structure</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Data_structure" title="Data structure">data structure</a> containing I-vars. A related synchronization construct that can be set multiple times with different values is called an <i>M-var</i>. M-vars support atomic operations to <i>take</i> or <i>put</i> the current value, where taking the value also sets the M-var back to its initial <i>empty</i> state.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <i>concurrent logic variable</i><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="It is not obvious to the reader where a definition and description of a concurrent logic variable can be found. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> is similar to a future, but is updated by <a href="/wiki/Unification_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unification (computing)">unification</a>, in the same way as <i>logic variables</i> in <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic programming</a>. Thus it can be bound more than once to unifiable values, but cannot be set back to an empty or unresolved state. The dataflow variables of Oz act as concurrent logic variables, and also have blocking semantics as mentioned above.</p>
<p>A <i>concurrent constraint variable</i> is a generalization of concurrent logic variables to support <a href="/wiki/Constraint_logic_programming" title="Constraint logic programming">constraint logic programming</a>: the constraint may be <i>narrowed</i> multiple times, indicating smaller sets of possible values. Typically there is a way to specify a thunk that should run whenever the constraint is narrowed further; this is needed to support <i>constraint propagation</i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_between_the_expressiveness_of_different_forms_of_future">Relations between the expressiveness of different forms of future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Relations between the expressiveness of different forms of future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Eager thread-specific futures can be straightforwardly implemented in non-thread-specific futures, by creating a thread to calculate the value at the same time as creating the future. In this case it is desirable to return a read-only view to the client, so that only the newly created thread is able to resolve this future.</p>
<p>To implement implicit lazy thread-specific futures (as provided by Alice ML, for example) in terms in non-thread-specific futures, needs a mechanism to determine when the future's value is first needed (for example, the <code>WaitNeeded</code> construct in Oz<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>). If all values are objects, then the ability to implement transparent forwarding objects is sufficient, since the first message sent to the forwarder indicates that the future's value is needed.</p>
<p>Non-thread-specific futures can be implemented in thread-specific futures, assuming that the system supports message passing, by having the resolving thread send a message to the future's own thread. However, this can be viewed as unneeded complexity. In programming languages based on threads, the most expressive approach seems to be to provide a mix of non-thread-specific futures, read-only views, and either a <i>WaitNeeded</i> construct, or support for transparent forwarding.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evaluation_strategy">Evaluation strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Evaluation strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Call_by_future" class="mw-redirect" title="Call by future">Call by future</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Evaluation_strategy" title="Evaluation strategy">evaluation strategy</a> of futures, which may be termed <i><a href="/wiki/Call_by_future" class="mw-redirect" title="Call by future">call by future</a></i>, is non-deterministic: the value of a future will be evaluated at some time between when the future is created and when its value is used, but the precise time is not determined beforehand and can change from run to run. The computation can start as soon as the future is created (<a href="/wiki/Eager_evaluation" title="Eager evaluation">eager evaluation</a>) or only when the value is actually needed (<a href="/wiki/Lazy_evaluation" title="Lazy evaluation">lazy evaluation</a>), and may be suspended part-way through, or executed in one run. Once the value of a future is assigned, it is not recomputed on future accesses; this is like the <a href="/wiki/Memoization" title="Memoization">memoization</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Call_by_need" class="mw-redirect" title="Call by need">call by need</a>.</p>
<p>A <b><span id="lazy_future">lazy future</span></b> is a future that deterministically has lazy evaluation semantics: the computation of the future's value starts when the value is first needed, as in call by need. Lazy futures are of use in languages which evaluation strategy is by default not lazy. For example, in <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B11" title="C++11">C++11</a> such lazy futures can be created by passing the <code>std::launch::deferred</code> launch policy to <code>std::async</code>, along with the function to compute the value.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Semantics_of_futures_in_the_actor_model">Semantics of futures in the actor model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Semantics of futures in the actor model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the actor model, an expression of the form <code><i>future</i> &lt;Expression&gt;</code> is defined by how it responds to an <code>Eval</code> message with environment <i>E</i> and customer <i>C</i> as follows: The future expression responds to the <code>Eval</code> message by sending the customer <i>C</i> a newly created actor <i>F</i> (the proxy for the response of evaluating <code>&lt;Expression&gt;</code>) as a return value <i>concurrently</i> with sending <code>&lt;Expression&gt;</code> an <code>Eval</code> message with environment <i>E</i> and customer <i>C</i>. The default behavior of <i>F</i> is as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>When <i>F</i> receives a request <i>R</i>, then it checks to see if it has already received a response (that can either be a return value or a thrown exception) from evaluating <code>&lt;Expression&gt;</code> proceeding as follows:
<ol>
<li>If it already has a response <i>V</i>, then
<ul>
<li>If <i>V</i> is a return value, then it is sent the request <i>R</i>.</li>
<li>If <i>V</i> is an exception, then it is thrown to the customer of the request <i>R</i>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>If it does not already have a response, then <i>R</i> is stored in the queue of requests inside the <i>F</i>.</li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>When <i>F</i> receives the response <i>V</i> from evaluating <code>&lt;Expression&gt;</code>, then <i>V</i> is stored in <i>F</i> and
<ul>
<li>If <i>V</i> is a return value, then all of the queued requests are sent to <i>V</i>.</li>
<li>If <i>V</i> is an exception, then it is thrown to the customer of each of the queued requests.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>However, some futures can deal with requests in special ways to provide greater parallelism. For example, the expression <code>1 + future factorial(n)</code> can create a new future that will behave like the number <code>1+factorial(n)</code>. This trick does not always work. For example, the following conditional expression:</p>
<dl>
<dd><code><i>if</i> m&gt;future factorial(n) <i>then</i> print("bigger") <i>else</i> print("smaller")</code></dd>
</dl>
<p>suspends until the future for <code>factorial(n)</code> has responded to the request asking if <code>m</code> is greater than itself.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>future</i> and/or <i>promise</i> constructs were first implemented in programming languages such as <a href="/wiki/MultiLisp" title="MultiLisp">MultiLisp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actor_model" title="Actor model">Act 1</a>. The use of logic variables for communication in <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">concurrent</a> <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic programming</a> languages was quite similar to futures. These began in <i>Prolog with Freeze</i> and <i>IC Prolog</i>, and became a true concurrency primitive with Relational Language, Concurrent <a href="/wiki/Prolog" title="Prolog">Prolog</a>, guarded <a href="/wiki/Horn_clause" title="Horn clause">Horn clauses</a> (GHC), <a href="/wiki/Parlog" title="Parlog">Parlog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strand_(programming_language)" title="Strand (programming language)">Strand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(programming_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vulcan (programming language)">Vulcan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janus_(concurrent_constraint_programming_language)" title="Janus (concurrent constraint programming language)">Janus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oz_(programming_language)" title="Oz (programming language)">Oz-Mozart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flow_Java" class="mw-redirect" title="Flow Java">Flow Java</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alice_(programming_language)" title="Alice (programming language)">Alice ML</a>. The single-assignment <i>I-var</i> from <a href="/wiki/Dataflow_programming" title="Dataflow programming">dataflow programming</a> languages, originating in <a href="/wiki/Id_(programming_language)" title="Id (programming language)">Id</a> and included in Reppy's <i><a href="/wiki/Concurrent_ML" title="Concurrent ML">Concurrent ML</a></i>, is much like the concurrent logic variable.</p>
<p>The promise pipelining technique (using futures to overcome latency) was invented by <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Liskov" title="Barbara Liskov">Barbara Liskov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liuba_Shrira" title="Liuba Shrira">Liuba Shrira</a> in 1988,<sup id="cite_ref-SIGPLAN_pp._260_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SIGPLAN_pp._260-7">[7]</a></sup> and independently by <a href="/wiki/Mark_S._Miller" title="Mark S. Miller">Mark S. Miller</a>, Dean Tribble and Rob Jellinghaus in the context of <a href="/wiki/Project_Xanadu" title="Project Xanadu">Project Xanadu</a> circa 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term <i>promise</i> was coined by Liskov and Shrira, although they referred to the pipelining mechanism by the name <i>call-stream</i>, which is now rarely used.</p>
<p>Both the design described in Liskov and Shrira's paper, and the implementation of promise pipelining in Xanadu, had the limit that promise values were not <a href="/wiki/First-class_value" class="mw-redirect" title="First-class value">first-class</a>: an argument to, or the value returned by a call or send could not directly be a promise (so the example of promise pipelining given earlier, which uses a promise for the result of one send as an argument to another, would not have been directly expressible in the call-stream design or in the Xanadu implementation). It seems that promises and call-streams were never implemented in any public release of Argus,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> the programming language used in the Liskov and Shrira paper. Argus development stopped around 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The Xanadu implementation of promise pipelining only became publicly available with the release of the source code for Udanax Gold<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> in 1999, and was never explained in any published document.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The later implementations in Joule and E support fully first-class promises and resolvers.</p>
<p>Several early actor languages, including the Act series,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> supported both parallel message passing and pipelined message processing, but not promise pipelining. (Although it is technically possible to implement the last of these features in the first two, there is no evidence that the Act languages did so.)</p>
<p>After 2000, a major revival of interest in futures and promises occurred, due to their use in <a href="/wiki/Responsiveness" title="Responsiveness">responsiveness</a> of user interfaces, and in <a href="/wiki/Web_development" title="Web development">web development</a>, due to the <a href="/wiki/Request%E2%80%93response" title="Request–response">request–response</a> model of message-passing. Several mainstream languages now have language support for futures and promises, most notably popularized by the <code>async</code> and <code>await</code> constructions in .NET 4.5 (announced 2010, released 2012)<sup id="cite_ref-asyncdotnet45_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asyncdotnet45-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dotnetasyncawait_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dotnetasyncawait-23">[23]</a></sup> largely inspired by the <i>asynchronous workflows</i> of F#,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> which dates to 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This has subsequently been adopted by other languages, notably Dart (2014),<sup id="cite_ref-dart_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dart-26">[26]</a></sup> Python (2015),<sup id="cite_ref-python_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-python-27">[27]</a></sup> Hack (HHVM), and drafts of ECMAScript 7 (JavaScript), Scala, and C++.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_implementations">List of implementations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: List of implementations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Languages supporting futures, promises, concurrent logic variables, dataflow variables, or I-vars, either by direct language support or in the standard library, include:</p>

<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=FreeFuture(DSL_library)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="FreeFuture(DSL library) (page does not exist)">FreeFuture</a> via <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://github.com/politrons/FreeFuture">FreeFuture</a></li>
<li>ABCL/f<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alice_(programming_language)" title="Alice (programming language)">Alice ML</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/AmbientTalk" title="AmbientTalk">AmbientTalk</a> (including first-class resolvers and read-only promises)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>, starting with <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B11#Threading_facilities" title="C++11">C++11</a>: std::future and std::promise
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/%CE%9CC%2B%2B" title="ΜC++">μC++</a></li>

<li>Some <a href="/wiki/Lisp_(programming_language)" title="Lisp (programming language)">Lisps</a>
<ul>

<li><a href="/wiki/Swift_(programming_language)" title="Swift (programming language)">Swift</a> (only via third party libraries)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Visual_Basic.NET" class="mw-redirect" title="Visual Basic.NET">Visual Basic</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> 11 (via the keywords <i>Async</i> and <i>Await</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-dotnetasyncawait_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dotnetasyncawait-23">[23]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Languages also supporting promise pipelining include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/E_(programming_language)" title="E (programming language)">E</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joule_(programming_language)" title="Joule (programming language)">Joule</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Non-standard library based implementations of futures:</p>
<ul>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Common_Lisp" title="Common Lisp">Common Lisp</a>:
<ul>
<li>Blackbird<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>Eager Future2<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li>lparallel<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>PCall<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For C++:
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Boost_(C%2B%2B_libraries)" title="Boost (C++ libraries)">Boost library</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dlib" title="Dlib">Dlib</a><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qt_(software)" title="Qt (software)">Qt</a><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Seastar" class="mw-redirect" title="Seastar">Seastar</a><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>Folly<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/POCO_C%2B%2B_Libraries" title="POCO C++ Libraries">POCO C++ Libraries</a> (Active Results)<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language)" title="C Sharp (programming language)">C#</a> and other <a href="/wiki/.NET_Framework" title=".NET Framework">.NET</a> languages: The <a href="/wiki/Parallel_Extensions" title="Parallel Extensions">Parallel Extensions</a> library</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Groovy_(programming_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Groovy (programming language)">Groovy</a>: GPars<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>:
<ul>
<li>Cujo.js'<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> when.js <sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> provides promises conforming to the Promises/A+<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> 1.1 specification</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Dojo_Toolkit" title="Dojo Toolkit">Dojo Toolkit</a> supplies promises<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Twisted_(software)" title="Twisted (software)">Twisted</a> style deferreds</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/MochiKit" title="MochiKit">MochiKit</a><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> inspired by <a href="/wiki/Twisted_(software)#Deferreds" title="Twisted (software)">Twisted's Deferreds</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jquery.com/">jQuery's</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//api.jquery.com/category/deferred-object/">Deferred Object</a> is based on the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://wiki.commonjs.org/wiki/Promises/A">CommonJS Promises/A</a> design.</li>
<li>Angularjs<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>node-promise<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>Q, by Kris Kowal, conforms to Promises/A+ 1.1<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>RSVP.js, conforms to Promises/A+ 1.1<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>YUI's<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> promise class<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> conforms to the Promises/A+ 1.0 specification.</li>
<li>Bluebird, by Petka Antonov<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Google_Closure_Tools#Closure_Library" title="Google Closure Tools">Closure Library</a>'s <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://github.com/google/closure-library/tree/master/closure/goog/promise">promise</a> package conforms to the Promises/A+ specification.</li>
<li>See <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://promisesaplus.com/implementations">Promise/A+'s</a> list for more implementations based on the Promise/A+ design.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>:
<ul>
<li>JDeferred, provides deferred-promise API and behavior similar to <a href="/wiki/JQuery" title="JQuery">JQuery</a>.Deferred object<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li>ParSeq<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> provides task-promise API ideal for asynchronous pipelining and branching, maintained by <a href="/wiki/LinkedIn" title="LinkedIn">LinkedIn</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Objective-C" title="Objective-C">Objective-C</a>: MAFuture,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> RXPromise,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> ObjC-CollapsingFutures,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> PromiseKit,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> objc-promise,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> OAPromise,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/OCaml" title="OCaml">OCaml</a>: Lazy module implements lazy explicit futures<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a>: Future,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Promises,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and Reflex<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">PHP</a>: React/Promise<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>:
<ul>
<li>Built-in implementation<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li>pythonfutures<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Twisted_(software)" title="Twisted (software)">Twisted's</a> Deferreds<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/R_(programming_language)" title="R (programming language)">R</a>:
<ul>
<li>future, implements an extendable future API with lazy and eager synchronous and multicore asynchronous futures<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language)" title="Ruby (programming language)">Ruby</a>:
<ul>
<li>Promise gem<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>libuv gem, implements promises<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li>Celluloid gem, implements futures<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li>future-resource<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Rust_(programming_language)" title="Rust (programming language)">Rust</a>:
<ul>
<li>futures-rs<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Swift_(programming_language)" title="Swift (programming language)">Swift</a>:
<ul>
<li>Async framework, implements C#-style <code>async</code>/non-blocking <code>await</code><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li>FutureKit,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> implements a version for Apple GCD<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li>FutureLib, pure Swift 2 library implementing Scala-style futures and promises with TPL-style cancellation<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Deferred, pure Swift library inspired by OCaml's Deferred<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li>BrightFutures<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Tcl_(programming_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tcl (programming language)">Tcl</a>: tcl-promise<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coroutines">Coroutines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Coroutines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Futures can be implemented in <a href="/wiki/Coroutine" title="Coroutine">coroutines</a><sup id="cite_ref-python_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-python-27">[27]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Generator_(computer_programming)" title="Generator (computer programming)">generators</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> resulting in the same evaluation strategy (e.g., cooperative multitasking or lazy evaluation).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Channels">Channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futures_and_promises&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Channel_(programming)" title="Channel (programming)">Channel (programming)</a></div>
<p>Futures can easily be implemented in <a href="/wiki/Channel_(programming)" title="Channel (programming)">channels</a>: a future is a one-element channel, and a promise is a process that sends to the channel, fulfilling the future.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> This allows futures to be implemented in concurrent programming languages with support for channels, such as CSP and <a href="/wiki/Go_(programming_language)" title="Go (programming language)">Go</a>. The resulting futures are explicit, as they must be accessed by reading from the channel, rather than only evaluation.</p>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://wiki.erights.org/wiki/Promise"><i>Promise</i></a>, E rights</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Promise&amp;rft.pub=E+rights&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwiki.erights.org%2Fwiki%2FPromise&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFutures+and+promises" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://aosabook.org/en/500L/a-web-crawler-with-asyncio-coroutines.html#fn13">500 lines or less, "A Web Crawler With asyncio Coroutines" by A. Jesse Jiryu Davis and Guido van Rossum</a> says "implementation uses an asyncio.Event in place of the Future shown here. The difference is an Event can be reset, whereas a Future cannot transition from resolved back to pending."</span></li>

<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Continuation-passing style</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
								<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p>In <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional programming</a>, <b>continuation-passing style</b> (<b>CPS</b>) is a style of programming in which <a href="/wiki/Control_flow" title="Control flow">control</a> is passed explicitly in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Continuation" title="Continuation">continuation</a>. This is contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Direct_style" title="Direct style">direct style</a>, which is the usual style of programming. <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Jay_Sussman" title="Gerald Jay Sussman">Gerald Jay Sussman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guy_L._Steele,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Guy L. Steele, Jr.">Guy L. Steele, Jr.</a> coined the phrase in <a href="/wiki/AI_Memo" title="AI Memo">AI Memo</a> 349 (1975), which sets out the first version of the <a href="/wiki/Scheme_(programming_language)" title="Scheme (programming language)">Scheme</a> programming language.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_C._Reynolds" title="John C. Reynolds">John C. Reynolds</a> gives a detailed account of the numerous discoveries of continuations.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>A function written in continuation-passing style takes an extra argument: an explicit "continuation", i.e. a function of one argument. When the CPS function has computed its result value, it "returns" it by calling the continuation function with this value as the argument. That means that when invoking a CPS function, the calling function is required to supply a procedure to be invoked with the subroutine's "return" value. Expressing code in this form makes a number of things explicit which are implicit in direct style. These include: procedure returns, which become apparent as calls to a continuation; intermediate values, which are all given names; order of argument evaluation, which is made explicit; and <a href="/wiki/Tail_call" title="Tail call">tail calls</a>, which simply call a procedure with the same continuation, unmodified, that was passed to the caller.</p>
<p>Programs can be automatically transformed from direct style to CPS. Functional and <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic</a> compilers often use CPS as an <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_representation" title="Intermediate representation">intermediate representation</a> where a compiler for an <a href="/wiki/Imperative_programming" title="Imperative programming">imperative</a> or <a href="/wiki/Procedural_programming" title="Procedural programming">procedural</a> <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming language</a> would use <a href="/wiki/Static_single_assignment_form" title="Static single assignment form">static single assignment form</a> (SSA).<sup id="cite_ref-Appel1998_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appel1998-4">[4]</a></sup> SSA is formally equivalent to a subset of CPS (excluding non-local control flow, which does not occur when CPS is used as intermediate representation).<sup id="cite_ref-Kelsey1995_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kelsey1995-5">[5]</a></sup> Functional compilers can also use <a href="/wiki/A-normal_form" title="A-normal form">A-normal form</a> (ANF) (but only for languages requiring eager evaluation), rather than with '<a href="/wiki/Thunk_(delayed_computation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thunk (delayed computation)">thunks</a>' (described in the examples below) in CPS. CPS is used more frequently by <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compilers</a> than by programmers as a local or global style.</p>
<p></p>
