The free <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Visual_Web_Developer_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsoft Visual Web Developer Express">Microsoft Visual Web Developer Express</a> provides a limited version of the JavaScript debugging functionality in Microsoft Visual Studio. Internet Explorer has included developer tools since version 8.</p>
<p>In comparison to Internet Explorer, Firefox has a more comprehensive set of developer tools, which include a debugger as well. Old versions of Firefox without these tools used a <a href="/wiki/Firefox_addon" class="mw-redirect" title="Firefox addon">Firefox addon</a> called <a href="/wiki/Firebug_(Firefox_extension)" class="mw-redirect" title="Firebug (Firefox extension)">Firebug</a>, or the older <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venkman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Venkman (page does not exist)">Venkman</a> debugger. Also, <a href="/wiki/WebKit" title="WebKit">WebKit</a>'s Web Inspector includes a JavaScript debugger,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> which is used in <a href="/wiki/Safari_(Web_browser)" class="mw-redirect" title="Safari (Web browser)">Safari</a>. A modified version called Blink DevTools is used in <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a>. <a href="/wiki/Node.js" title="Node.js">Node.js</a> has Node Inspector, an interactive debugger that integrates with the Blink DevTools, available in <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a>. <a href="/wiki/Opera_(Web_browser)" class="mw-redirect" title="Opera (Web browser)">Opera</a> includes a set of tools called <a href="/wiki/Opera_Dragonfly" title="Opera Dragonfly">Dragonfly</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Online_dev_tools"></span>In addition to the native computer software, there are online JavaScript IDEs, debugging aids that are themselves written in JavaScript and built to run on the Web. An example is the program <a href="/wiki/JSLint" title="JSLint">JSLint</a>, developed by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Crockford" title="Douglas Crockford">Douglas Crockford</a> who has written extensively on the language. JSLint scans JavaScript code for conformance to a set of standards and guidelines. Many libraries for JavaScript, such as <a href="/wiki/Three.js" title="Three.js">three.js</a>, provide links to demonstration code that can be edited by users. They are also used as a pedagogical tool by institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Khan_Academy" title="Khan Academy">Khan Academy</a><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> to allow students to experience writing code in an environment where they can see the output of their programs, without needing any setup beyond a Web browser.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Benchmark_tools_for_developers">Benchmark tools for developers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Benchmark tools for developers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since JavaScript is getting more important for web development (frontend overtakes many aspects which were done in backend before), there is also more consideration done about performance. Especially mobile devices could have problems with rendering and processing unoptimized complex logic.</p>
<p>A library for doing benchmarks is <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://benchmarkjs.com/">benchmark.js</a>. A benchmarking library that supports high-resolution timers and returns statistically significant results<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Need citation on statistically significant results (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>Another tool is <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jsben.ch">jsben.ch</a>. An online JavaScript benchmarking tool, where code snippets can be tested against each other.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Version_history">Version history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Version history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript#Versions" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript §&#160;Versions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript#Version_correspondence" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript §&#160;Version correspondence</a></div>
<p>JavaScript was initially developed in 1996 for use in the <a href="/wiki/Netscape_Navigator" title="Netscape Navigator">Netscape Navigator</a> Web browser. In the same year Microsoft released an implementation for Internet Explorer. This implementation was called <a href="/wiki/JScript" title="JScript">JScript</a> due to trademark issues. In 1997, the first standardized version of the language was released under the name <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript</a> in the first edition of the ECMA-252 standard. The explicit versioning and opt-in of language features was Mozilla-specific and has been removed. Firefox 4 was the last version which referred to a JavaScript version (1.8.5). With new editions of the ECMA-262 standard, JavaScript language features are now often mentioned with their initial definition in the ECMA-262 editions.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_languages_and_technologies">Related languages and technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Related languages and technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a>, or JavaScript Object Notation, is a general-purpose data interchange format that is defined as a subset of JavaScript's object literal syntax. Like much of JavaScript (regexps and anonymous functions as 1st class elements, closures, flexible classes, 'use strict'), <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a>, except for replacing <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a>'s key-value operator '=&gt;' by an <a href="/wiki/RFC_822" class="mw-redirect" title="RFC 822">RFC 822</a><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> inspired ':', is syntactically pure Perl.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/JQuery" title="JQuery">jQuery</a> is a popular JavaScript library designed to simplify <a href="/wiki/Document_Object_Model" title="Document Object Model">DOM</a>-oriented client-side HTML scripting along with offering cross-browser compatibility because various browsers respond differently to certain vanilla JavaScript code.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Underscore.js" title="Underscore.js">Underscore.js</a> is a utility JavaScript library for data manipulation that is used in both client-side and server-side network applications.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Angular_(application_platform)" title="Angular (application platform)">Angular</a> and <a href="/wiki/AngularJS" title="AngularJS">AngularJS</a> are <a href="/wiki/Web_application_framework" class="mw-redirect" title="Web application framework">web application frameworks</a> to use for developing <a href="/wiki/Single-page_application" title="Single-page application">single-page applications</a> and also cross-platform mobile apps.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/React_(JavaScript_library)" title="React (JavaScript library)">React (JavaScript library)</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Open-source" class="mw-redirect" title="Open-source">open-source</a> JavaScript library providing a views that is rendered using components specified as custom HTML tags.</p>
<p>Mozilla browsers currently support <a href="/wiki/LiveConnect" class="mw-redirect" title="LiveConnect">LiveConnect</a>, a feature that allows JavaScript and Java to intercommunicate on the Web. However, Mozilla-specific support for LiveConnect was scheduled to be phased out in the future in favor of passing on the LiveConnect handling via <a href="/wiki/NPAPI" title="NPAPI">NPAPI</a> to the Java 1.6+ plug-in (not yet supported on the Mac as of March 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Most browser inspection tools, such as <a href="/wiki/Firebug_(software)" title="Firebug (software)">Firebug</a> in Firefox, include JavaScript interpreters that can act on the visible page's DOM.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asm.js" title="Asm.js">asm.js</a> is a subset of JavaScript that can be run in any JavaScript engine or run faster in an <a href="/wiki/Ahead-of-time" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahead-of-time">ahead-of-time</a> (AOT) compiling engine.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/JSFuck" title="JSFuck">JSFuck</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_programming_language" title="Esoteric programming language">esoteric programming language</a>. Programs are written using only six different characters, but are still valid JavaScript code.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Processing_(programming_language)#p5.js" title="Processing (programming language)">p5.js</a><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> is an object oriented JavaScript library designed for artists and designers. It is based on the ideas of the <a href="/wiki/Processing_(programming_language)" title="Processing (programming language)">Processing</a> project but is for the web.</p>
<p><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jsben.ch">jsben.ch</a> is an online JavaScript benchmarking tool, where different code snippets can be tested against each other.</p>
<p><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dspace.thapar.edu:8080/jspui/handle/10266/4162">CRISP: A Strategy guiding Cloud Application Development for Beginners</a> is a strategy proposed by Ayush Sahu to develop optimized and secure JavaScript application to be used in mobiles, PC's and other devices. CRISP (Conversion, Reformat code, Isolate module, Sandbox, Partition) strategy has been proposed for refined conversion of native application to JavaScript for cloud application development. JavaScript is chosen as medium for writing application because it is mostly used language among developers and provides rich API (Application Programming Interface) for writing applications.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_an_intermediate_language">Use as an intermediate language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Use as an intermediate language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As JavaScript is the most widely supported client-side language that can run within a Web browser, it has become an <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermediate language">intermediate language</a> for other languages to target. This has included both newly created languages and ports of existing languages. Some of these include:</p>
<ul>
<li>OberonScript, a full implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(programming_language)" title="Oberon (programming language)">Oberon</a> programming language that compiles to high-level JavaScript.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Objective-J" title="Objective-J">Objective-J</a>, a superset of JavaScript that compiles to standard JavaScript. It adds traditional inheritance and <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a>/<a href="/wiki/Objective-C" title="Objective-C">Objective-C</a> style dynamic dispatch and optional pseudo-static typing to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Processing.js" title="Processing.js">Processing.js</a>, a JavaScript port of the Processing programming language designed to write visualizations, images, and interactive content. It allows Web browsers to display animations, visual applications, games and other graphical rich content without the need for a Java applet or Flash plugin.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/CoffeeScript" title="CoffeeScript">CoffeeScript</a>, an alternate syntax for JavaScript intended to be more concise and readable. It adds features like array comprehensions (also available in JavaScript since version 1.7)<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and pattern matching. Like Objective-J, it compiles to JavaScript. Ruby and Python have been cited as influential on CoffeeScript syntax.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Google_Web_Toolkit" title="Google Web Toolkit">Google Web Toolkit</a> translates a subset of Java to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scala_(programming_language)" title="Scala (programming language)">Scala</a>, an object-oriented and functional programming language, has a Scala-to-JavaScript compiler.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pyjs" title="Pyjs">Pyjs</a>, a port of <a href="/wiki/Google_Web_Toolkit" title="Google Web Toolkit">Google Web Toolkit</a> to <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> translates a subset of Python to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dart_(programming_language)" title="Dart (programming language)">Google Dart</a>, an all-purpose, <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open source</a> language that compiles to JavaScript.</li>
<li>Whalesong,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Racket_(programming_language)" title="Racket (programming language)">Racket</a>-to-JavaScript compiler.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Emscripten" title="Emscripten">Emscripten</a>, a <a href="/wiki/LLVM" title="LLVM">LLVM</a>-backend for porting native libraries to JavaScript, known as <a href="/wiki/Asm.js" title="Asm.js">asm.js</a><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fantom_(programming_language)" title="Fantom (programming language)">Fantom</a> a programming language that runs on JVM, .NET and JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/TypeScript" title="TypeScript">TypeScript</a>, a free and open-source programming language developed by Microsoft. It is a superset of JavaScript, and essentially adds support for optional <a href="/wiki/Type_annotation" class="mw-redirect" title="Type annotation">type annotations</a> and some other language extensions such as classes, interfaces and modules. A TS-script compiles into plain JavaScript and can be executed in any JS host supporting <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript</a> 3 or higher. The compiler is itself written in TypeScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elm_(programming_language)" title="Elm (programming language)">Elm (programming language)</a> is a pure functional language for web apps. Unlike handwritten JavaScript, Elm-generated JavaScript has zero runtime exceptions, a time-traveling debugger, and enforced semantic versioning.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haxe" title="Haxe">Haxe</a>, an open-source high-level multiplatform programming language and compiler that can produce applications and source code for many different platforms including JavaScript.</li>
<li>ClojureScript,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> a compiler for <a href="/wiki/Clojure" title="Clojure">Clojure</a> that targets JavaScript. It is designed to emit JavaScript code that is compatible with the advanced compilation mode of the Google Closure optimizing compiler.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://bertfreudenberg.github.io/SqueakJS/">SqueakJS</a>, a virtual machine and DOM environment for the open-source <a href="/wiki/Squeak" title="Squeak">Squeak</a> implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a> programming language.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Free_Pascal" title="Free Pascal">Free Pascal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> a compiler for <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a> that targets JavaScript.</li>
</ul>
<p><br />
As JavaScript has unusual limitations – such as no explicit integer type, only double-precision binary floating point – languages that compile to JavaScript and do not take care to use the integer-converting shift and bitwise logical operators may have slightly different behavior than in other environments.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="JavaScript_and_Java">JavaScript and Java</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: JavaScript and Java">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A common misconception is that JavaScript is similar or closely related to <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>. It is true that both have a C-like syntax (the C language being their most immediate common ancestor language). They also are both typically <a href="/wiki/Sandbox_(computer_security)" title="Sandbox (computer security)">sandboxed</a> (when used inside a browser), and JavaScript was designed with Java's syntax and standard library in mind. In particular, all Java keywords were reserved in original JavaScript, JavaScript's standard library follows Java's naming conventions, and JavaScript's <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="nb">Math</span></code> and <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="nb">Date</span></code> objects are based on classes from Java 1.0,<sup id="cite_ref-popularity_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popularity-125">[125]</a></sup> but the similarities end there.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> and JavaScript both first appeared in 1995, but Java was developed by <a href="/wiki/James_Gosling" title="James Gosling">James Gosling</a> of Sun Microsystems, and JavaScript by <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Eich" title="Brendan Eich">Brendan Eich</a> of Netscape Communications.</p>
<p>The differences between the two languages are more prominent than their similarities. Java has <a href="/wiki/Static_typing" class="mw-redirect" title="Static typing">static typing</a>, while JavaScript's typing is <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_typing" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamic typing">dynamic</a>. Java is loaded from compiled bytecode, while JavaScript is loaded as human-readable source code. Java's objects are <a href="/wiki/Class-based_programming" title="Class-based programming">class-based</a>, while JavaScript's are <a href="/wiki/Prototype-based_programming" title="Prototype-based programming">prototype-based</a>. Finally, Java did not support <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional programming</a> until Java 8, while JavaScript has done so from the beginning, being influenced by <a href="/wiki/Scheme_(programming_language)" title="Scheme (programming language)">Scheme</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="WebAssembly">WebAssembly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: WebAssembly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Starting in 2017, web browsers began supporting <a href="/wiki/WebAssembly" title="WebAssembly">WebAssembly</a>, a technology standardized by the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web_Consortium" title="World Wide Web Consortium">W3C</a>. The WebAssembly standard specifies a binary format, which can be produced by a compiler toolchain such as <a href="/wiki/LLVM" title="LLVM">LLVM</a>, to execute in the browser at near native speed. WebAssembly allows programming languages such as C, C++, C# and Java to be used as well as JavaScript to author client-side code for the World Wide Web.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">

<th scope="row" style="white-space: nowrap;">Website</th>
<td><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://nodejs.org">nodejs<wbr />.org</a></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Node.js</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open-source</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cross-platform" title="Cross-platform">cross-platform</a> <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a> <a href="/wiki/Runtime_system" title="Runtime system">run-time environment</a> that executes JavaScript code <a href="/wiki/Server-side" title="Server-side">server-side</a>. Historically, JavaScript was used primarily for <a href="/wiki/Client-side_scripting" class="mw-redirect" title="Client-side scripting">client-side scripting</a>, in which scripts written in JavaScript are embedded in a webpage's HTML and run client-side by a JavaScript engine in the user's web browser. Node.js lets developers use JavaScript for <a href="/wiki/Server-side_scripting" title="Server-side scripting">server-side scripting</a>—running scripts server-side to produce <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_web_page" title="Dynamic web page">dynamic web page</a> content <i>before</i> the page is sent to the user's web browser. Consequently, Node.js represents a "JavaScript everywhere" paradigm,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> unifying <a href="/wiki/Web_application" title="Web application">web application</a> development around a single programming language, rather than different languages for server side and client side scripts.</p>
<p>Though <code>.js</code> is the conventional <a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">filename extension</a> for JavaScript code, the name "Node.js" does not refer to a particular file in this context and is merely the name of the product. Node.js has an <a href="/wiki/Event-driven_architecture" title="Event-driven architecture">event-driven architecture</a> capable of <a href="/wiki/Asynchronous_I/O" title="Asynchronous I/O">asynchronous I/O</a>. These design choices aim to optimize <a href="/wiki/Throughput" title="Throughput">throughput</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scalability" title="Scalability">scalability</a> in web applications with many input/output operations, as well as for <a href="/wiki/Real-time_Web" class="mw-redirect" title="Real-time Web">real-time Web</a> applications (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Real-time_communication" title="Real-time communication">real-time communication</a> programs and <a href="/wiki/Browser_game" title="Browser game">browser games</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-readwrite_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-readwrite-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Node.js <a href="/wiki/Distributed_development" title="Distributed development">distributed development</a> project, governed by the Node.js Foundation,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> is facilitated by the <a href="/wiki/Linux_Foundation" title="Linux Foundation">Linux Foundation</a>'s Collaborative Projects program.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Corporate users of Node.js software include <a href="/wiki/GoDaddy" title="GoDaddy">GoDaddy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Groupon" title="Groupon">Groupon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/LinkedIn" title="LinkedIn">LinkedIn</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-vbliipad_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vbliipad-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-guardianMS_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianMS-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/PayPal" title="PayPal">PayPal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rakuten" title="Rakuten">Rakuten</a>, <a href="/wiki/SAP_SE" title="SAP SE">SAP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuenti" title="Tuenti">Tuenti</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-sapappbuilder_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sapappbuilder-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Voxer" title="Voxer">Voxer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walmart" title="Walmart">Walmart</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-vbwalmart_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vbwalmart-21">[21]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryan_Dahl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ryan_Dahl.jpg/220px-Ryan_Dahl.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ryan_Dahl.jpg/330px-Ryan_Dahl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ryan_Dahl.jpg/440px-Ryan_Dahl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="5184" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryan_Dahl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ryan Dahl, creator of Node.js, in 2010</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Node.js was originally written by <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Dahl" title="Ryan Dahl">Ryan Dahl</a> in 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-training.com_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-training.com-23">[23]</a></sup> about thirteen years after the introduction of the first server-side JavaScript environment, Netscape's LiveWire Pro Web.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The initial release supported only Linux and Mac OS X. Its development and maintenance was led by Dahl and later sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Joyent" title="Joyent">Joyent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Google_Groups_post_by_Ryan_Dahl_about_Joyent_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Google_Groups_post_by_Ryan_Dahl_about_Joyent-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dahl was inspired to create Node.js after seeing a file upload <a href="/wiki/Progress_bar" title="Progress bar">progress bar</a> on <a href="/wiki/Flickr" title="Flickr">Flickr</a>. The browser did not know how much of the file had been uploaded and had to query the Web server. Dahl desired an easier way.<sup id="cite_ref-readwrite_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-readwrite-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dahl criticized the limited possibilities of the most popular web server in 2009, <a href="/wiki/Apache_HTTP_Server" title="Apache HTTP Server">Apache HTTP Server</a>, to handle a lot of concurrent connections (up to 10,000 and more) and the most common way of creating code (sequential programming), when code either blocked the entire process or implied multiple execution stacks in the case of simultaneous connections.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dahl demonstrated the project at the inaugural European JSConf on November 8, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-b3_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b3-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Node.js combined <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s V8 JavaScript engine, an <a href="/wiki/Event_loop" title="Event loop">event loop</a>, and a low-level <a href="/wiki/Input/output" title="Input/output">I/O</a> <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">API</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2010, a <a href="/wiki/Package_manager" title="Package manager">package manager</a> was introduced for the Node.js environment called <a href="/wiki/Npm_(software)" title="Npm (software)"><i>npm</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The package manager makes it easier for programmers to publish and share source code of Node.js libraries and is designed to simplify installation, updating, and uninstallation of libraries.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2011, Microsoft and Joyent implemented a native <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a> version of Node.js.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The first Node.js build supporting Windows was released in July 2011.</p>
<p>In January 2012, Dahl stepped aside, promoting coworker and <i>npm</i> creator Isaac Schlueter to manage the project.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In January 2014, Schlueter announced that Timothy J. Fontaine would lead the project.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2014, Fedor Indutny started io.js, a <a href="/wiki/Fork_(software_development)" title="Fork (software development)">fork</a> of Node.js. Due to the internal conflict over Joyent's governance, io.js was created as an <a href="/wiki/Open_governance" class="mw-redirect" title="Open governance">open governance</a> alternative with a separate technical committee.<sup id="cite_ref-Krill,_Paul_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krill,_Paul-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iwtw_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iwtw-37">[37]</a></sup> Unlike Node.js,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> the authors planned to keep io.js up-to-date with the latest releases of the <a href="/wiki/V8_(JavaScript_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="V8 (JavaScript engine)">Google V8</a> JavaScript engine.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikeal,_Rogers_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikeal,_Rogers-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2015, the intent to form a neutral Node.js Foundation was announced. By June 2015, the Node.js and io.js communities voted to work together under the Node.js Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2015, Node.js v0.12 and io.js v3.3 were merged back together into Node v4.0.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> This brought V8 <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript#ES6" title="ECMAScript">ES6</a> features into Node.js, and a long-term support release cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> As of 2016, the io.js website recommends that developers switch back to Node.js and that no further releases of io.js are planned due to the merger.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Node.js allows the creation of <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">Web servers</a> and networking tools using <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a> and a collection of "modules" that handle various core functionality.<sup id="cite_ref-b3_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b3-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b2_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b2-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b4_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b4-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b5_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b5-46">[46]</a></sup> Modules are provided for <a href="/wiki/File_system" title="File system">file system</a> I/O, networking (<a href="/wiki/Domain_Name_System" title="Domain Name System">DNS</a>, <a href="/wiki/HTTP" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP">HTTP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol" title="Transmission Control Protocol">TCP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">TLS/SSL</a>, or <a href="/wiki/User_Datagram_Protocol" title="User Datagram Protocol">UDP</a>), <a href="/wiki/Binary_file" title="Binary file">binary</a> data (buffers), <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptography</a> functions, <a href="/wiki/Stream_(computing)" title="Stream (computing)">data streams</a>, and other core functions.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b4_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b4-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Node.js's modules use an API designed to reduce the complexity of writing server applications.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b4_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b4-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Node.js applications can run on <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">macOS</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>, <a href="/wiki/NonStop" class="mw-redirect" title="NonStop">NonStop</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bomBora_-_Node.js_for_NonStop_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bomBora_-_Node.js_for_NonStop-48">[48]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> servers. Alternatively, they can be written with <a href="/wiki/CoffeeScript" title="CoffeeScript">CoffeeScript</a><sup id="cite_ref-b6_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b6-49">[49]</a></sup> (a JavaScript alternative), <a href="/wiki/Dart_(programming_language)" title="Dart (programming language)">Dart</a> or <a href="/wiki/TypeScript" title="TypeScript">TypeScript</a> (<a href="/wiki/Strongly_typed" class="mw-redirect" title="Strongly typed">strongly typed</a> forms of JavaScript), or any other language that <a href="/wiki/Source-to-source_compiler" title="Source-to-source compiler">can compile to JavaScript</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-b6_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b6-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Node.js is primarily used to build network programs such as Web servers.<sup id="cite_ref-b2_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b2-44">[44]</a></sup> The biggest difference between Node.js and <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">PHP</a> is that most functions in PHP <a href="/wiki/Asynchronous_I/O" title="Asynchronous I/O">block</a> until completion (commands execute only after previous commands finish), while Node.js functions are <a href="/wiki/Asynchronous_I/O" title="Asynchronous I/O">non-blocking</a> (commands execute <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrently</a> or even in <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">parallel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-node_cluster_module_doc_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-node_cluster_module_doc-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and use <a href="/wiki/Callback_(computer_programming)" title="Callback (computer programming)">callbacks</a> to signal completion or failure).<sup id="cite_ref-b2_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b2-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Platform_architecture">Platform architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Platform architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Node.js brings <a href="/wiki/Event-driven_programming" title="Event-driven programming">event-driven programming</a> to <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">web servers</a>, enabling development of fast web servers in JavaScript.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup> Developers can create highly scalable servers without using <a href="/wiki/Thread_(computing)" title="Thread (computing)">threading</a>, by using a simplified model of <a href="/wiki/Event-driven_programming" title="Event-driven programming">event-driven programming</a> that uses callbacks to signal the completion of a task.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup> Node.js connects the ease of a scripting language (JavaScript) with the power of Unix network programming.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Node.js was built on the <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> <a href="/wiki/V8_JavaScript_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="V8 JavaScript engine">V8 JavaScript engine</a> since it was open-sourced under the <a href="/wiki/BSD_license" class="mw-redirect" title="BSD license">BSD license</a>. It is extremely fast and proficient with internet fundamentals such as <a href="/wiki/HTTP" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP">HTTP</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNS" class="mw-redirect" title="DNS">DNS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol" title="Transmission Control Protocol">TCP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-b3_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b3-28">[28]</a></sup> Also, <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a> was a well-known language, making Node.js immediately accessible to the entire <a href="/wiki/Web_developer" title="Web developer">web development community</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-b3_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b3-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_support">Industry support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Industry support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are thousands of open-source libraries for Node.js, most of them hosted on the <a href="/wiki/Npm_(software)" title="Npm (software)">npm</a> website. The Node.js developer community has two main mailing lists and the <a href="/wiki/IRC" class="mw-redirect" title="IRC">IRC</a> channel #node.js on <a href="/wiki/Freenode" title="Freenode">freenode</a>. There are multiple developer conferences and events that support the Node.js community including <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://nodeconf.com/">NodeConf</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://events.linuxfoundation.org/events/node-interactive">Node Interactive</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nodesummit.com/">Node Summit</a> as well as a number of regional events.</p>
<p>The open-source community has developed <a href="/wiki/Web_framework" title="Web framework">web frameworks</a> to accelerate the development of applications. Such frameworks include Connect, <a href="/wiki/Express.js" title="Express.js">Express.js</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socket.IO" title="Socket.IO">Socket.IO</a>, Koa.js, Hapi.js, <a href="/wiki/Sails.js" title="Sails.js">Sails.js</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteor_(web_framework)" title="Meteor (web framework)">Meteor</a>, Derby, and many others.<sup id="cite_ref-b1_31-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b1-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b7_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b7-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern desktop <a href="/wiki/Integrated_development_environment" title="Integrated development environment">IDEs</a> provide editing and debugging features specifically for Node.js applications. Such IDEs include <a href="/wiki/Atom_(text_editor)" title="Atom (text editor)">Atom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brackets_(text_editor)" title="Brackets (text editor)">Brackets</a>, <a href="/wiki/JetBrains_MPS" title="JetBrains MPS">JetBrains</a> <a href="/wiki/WebStorm" class="mw-redirect" title="WebStorm">WebStorm</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Visual_Studio" title="Microsoft Visual Studio">Microsoft Visual Studio</a> (with Node.js Tools for Visual Studio,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/TypeScript" title="TypeScript">TypeScript</a> with Node definitions,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>) <a href="/wiki/NetBeans" title="NetBeans">NetBeans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nodeclipse" title="Nodeclipse">Nodeclipse</a> Enide Studio <sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Eclipse_(software)" title="Eclipse (software)">Eclipse</a>-based), and <a href="/wiki/Visual_Studio_Code" title="Visual Studio Code">Visual Studio Code</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Certain online web-based IDEs also support Node.js, such as <a href="/wiki/Codeanywhere" title="Codeanywhere">Codeanywhere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codenvy" class="mw-redirect" title="Codenvy">Codenvy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud9_IDE" title="Cloud9 IDE">Cloud9 IDE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koding" title="Koding">Koding</a>, and the visual flow editor in <a href="/wiki/Node-RED" title="Node-RED">Node-RED</a>.</p>

<p>New major releases of Node.js are cut from the <a href="/wiki/GitHub" title="GitHub">GitHub</a> master branch every six months. Even-numbered versions are cut in April and odd-numbered versions are cut in October. When a new odd version is released, the previous even version undergoes transition to Long Term Support (LTS), which gives that version 18 months of active support from the date it is designated LTS. After these 18 months expire, an LTS release receives an additional 12 months of maintenance support. An active version receives non-breaking backports of changes a few weeks after they land in the current release. A maintenance release only receives critical fixes and documentation updates.<sup id="cite_ref-Release_65-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Release-65">[65]</a></sup> The LTS Working Group manages strategy and policy in collaboration with the Technical Steering Committee of the Node.js Foundation.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_details">Technical details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Technical details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-technical" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may be too technical for most readers to understand</b>. Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Make_technical_articles_understandable" title="Wikipedia:Make technical articles understandable">make it understandable to non-experts</a>, without removing the technical details. <small><i>(August 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Node.js is a Javascript runtime environment that processes incoming requests in a loop, called the <a href="/wiki/Event_loop" title="Event loop">event loop</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Threading">Threading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Threading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Node.js operates on a <a href="/wiki/Single_threading" class="mw-redirect" title="Single threading">single thread</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Non-blocking_I/O" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-blocking I/O">non-blocking I/O</a> calls, allowing it to support tens of thousands of concurrent connections without incurring the cost of thread <a href="/wiki/Context_switch" title="Context switch">context switching</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The design of sharing a single thread among all the requests that use the <a href="/wiki/Observer_pattern" title="Observer pattern">observer pattern</a> is intended for building highly concurrent applications, where any function performing I/O must use a <a href="/wiki/Callback_(computer_programming)" title="Callback (computer programming)">callback</a>. To accommodate the single-threaded event loop, Node.js uses the <a href="/wiki/Libuv" title="Libuv">libuv</a> library—which, in turn, uses a fixed-sized thread pool that handles some of the non-blocking asynchronous I/O operations.<sup id="cite_ref-readwrite_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-readwrite-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>A downside of this single-threaded approach is that Node.js doesn't allow <a href="/wiki/Vertical_scaling" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertical scaling">vertical scaling</a> by increasing the number of <a href="/wiki/CPU_core" class="mw-redirect" title="CPU core">CPU cores</a> of the machine it is running on without using an additional module, such as cluster,<sup id="cite_ref-node_cluster_module_doc_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-node_cluster_module_doc-51">[51]</a></sup> StrongLoop Process Manager,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> or pm2.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> However, developers can increase the default number of threads in the libuv thread pool. The server <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system (OS)</a> is likely to distribute these threads across multiple cores.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>A thread pool handles execution of parallel tasks in Node.js. The main thread call functions post tasks to the shared task queue, which threads in the thread pool pull and execute. Inherently non-blocking system functions such as networking translate to kernel-side non-blocking sockets, while inherently blocking system functions such as file I/O run in a blocking way on their own threads. When a thread in the thread pool completes a task, it informs the main thread of this, which in turn, wakes up and executes the registered callback. Since Node.js handles callbacks in serial on the main thread, long lasting computations and other CPU-bound tasks freeze the entire event-loop until completion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="V8">V8</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: V8">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chrome_V8" title="Chrome V8">V8</a> is the JavaScript execution engine built for <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> and open-sourced by Google in 2008. Written in <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>, V8 compiles JavaScript source code to native <a href="/wiki/Machine_code" title="Machine code">machine code</a> instead of interpreting it in real time.<sup id="cite_ref-readwrite_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-readwrite-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Node.js uses <a href="/wiki/Libuv" title="Libuv">libuv</a> to handle asynchronous events. Libuv is an abstraction layer for network and file system functionality on both Windows and <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">POSIX</a>-based systems such as Linux, macOS, OSS on NonStop, and Unix.</p>
<p>The core functionality of Node.js resides in a JavaScript library. The Node.js bindings, written in C++, connect these technologies to each other and to the operating system.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Package_management">Package management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Package management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Npm_(software)" title="Npm (software)">npm</a> is the pre-installed package manager for the Node.js server platform. It installs Node.js programs from the npm registry, organizing the installation and management of third-party Node.js programs. Packages in the npm registry can range from simple helper libraries such as <a href="/wiki/Lodash" title="Lodash">Lodash</a> to task runners such as <a href="/wiki/Grunt_(software)" title="Grunt (software)">Grunt</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unified_API">Unified API</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Unified API">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Node.js can be combined with a browser, a database that supports JSON data (such as <a href="/wiki/PostgreSQL" title="PostgreSQL">Postgres</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/MongoDB" title="MongoDB">MongoDB</a>, or <a href="/wiki/CouchDB" class="mw-redirect" title="CouchDB">CouchDB</a>) and <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a> for a unified JavaScript development stack. With the adaptation of what were essentially server-side development patterns such as <a href="/wiki/Model%E2%80%93view%E2%80%93controller" title="Model–view–controller">MVC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Model_View_Presenter" class="mw-redirect" title="Model View Presenter">MVP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Model_View_ViewModel" class="mw-redirect" title="Model View ViewModel">MVVM</a>, etc., Node.js allows the <a href="/wiki/Code_reuse" title="Code reuse">reuse</a> of the same model and service interface between client-side and server-side.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Event_loop">Event loop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Event loop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Node.js registers with the operating system so the OS notifies it of connections and issues a callback. Within the Node.js runtime, each connection is a small <a href="/wiki/Heap_allocation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heap allocation">heap allocation</a>. Traditionally, relatively heavyweight OS processes or threads handled each connection. Node.js uses an event loop for scalability, instead of processes or threads.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In contrast to other event-driven servers, Node.js's event loop does not need to be called explicitly. Instead callbacks are defined, and the server automatically enters the event loop at the end of the callback definition. Node.js exits the event loop when there are no further callbacks to be performed.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_governance">Project governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Project governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2015, various branches of the greater Node.js community began working under the vendor-neutral Node.js Foundation. The stated purpose of the organization "is to enable widespread adoption and help accelerate development of Node.js and other related modules through an open governance model that encourages participation, technical contribution, and a framework for long-term stewardship by an ecosystem invested in Node.js' success."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Node.js Foundation Technical Steering Committee (TSC) is the technical governing body of the Node.js Foundation. The TSC is responsible for the core Node.js repo as well as dependent and adjacent projects. Generally the TSC delegates administration of these projects to working groups or committees.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The LTS group that manages long term supported releases is one such group. Other current groups include: Website, Streams, Build, Diagnostics, i18n, Evangelism, Docker, Addon API, Benchmarking, Post-mortem, Intl, Documentation, and Testing.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August, 2017, a governance breakdown precipitated efforts to repair the resulting damage and to create a path to future stability of governance.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives">Alternatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Node.js&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Alternatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A fork called <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://github.com/jxcore/jxcore">JXcore</a> was created in January 2014, it gave people the choice between Google's <a href="/wiki/Chrome_V8" title="Chrome V8">V8</a> and Mozilla's <a href="/wiki/SpiderMonkey" title="SpiderMonkey">SpiderMonkey</a> JavaScript engines and it was possible to run Node.js applications on Android and iOS mobile devices. The development was stopped in March 2016 and focused on <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://github.com/janeasystems/nodejs-mobile">nodejs-mobile</a>, a <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://github.com/nodejs/node-chakracore">node-chakracore</a> fork using Microsoft's <a href="/wiki/ChakraCore" class="mw-redirect" title="ChakraCore">ChakraCore</a> JavaScript engine. Another fork, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://github.com/mozilla/spidernode">spidernode</a> is inspired by node-chakracore but uses SpiderMonkey JavaScript engine instead.</p>
<p>Similar open source event-driven server frameworks for other platforms include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/EventMachine" title="EventMachine">EventMachine</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language)" title="Ruby (programming language)">Ruby</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Libuv" title="Libuv">libuv</a> for <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Perl_Object_Environment" title="Perl Object Environment">Perl Object Environment</a> for <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a></li>
<li>ReactPHP (libev or libevent) or Amp (libev, libevent, or libuv) for <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">PHP</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Twisted_(software)" title="Twisted (software)">Twisted</a> for <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vert.x" title="Vert.x">Vert.x</a> for <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>, <a href="/wiki/Groovy_(programming_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Groovy (programming language)">Apache Groovy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language)" title="Ruby (programming language)">Ruby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scala_(programming_language)" title="Scala (programming language)">Scala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clojure" title="Clojure">Clojure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ceylon_(programming_language)" title="Ceylon (programming language)">Ceylon</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Node.js may utilize code written in other programming languages using:</p>
<ul>
<li>Edge.js allows <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_.NET" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsoft .NET">Microsoft .NET</a> applications to run Node.js scripts in-process, and allows Node.js servers to utilize .NET compiled code via async callbacks.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>Luvit implements the Node.js APIs for the language <a href="/wiki/Lua_(programming_language)" title="Lua (programming language)">Lua</a><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>Node-julia allows using <a href="/wiki/Julia_(programming_language)" title="Julia (programming language)">Julia</a> with Node.js/io.js</li>
<li>The COBOL bridge for Node.js allows using <a href="/wiki/COBOL" title="COBOL">COBOL</a> with Node.js<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/FreePascal" class="mw-redirect" title="FreePascal">FreePascal</a> 3.0.4 contains an Object Pascal to JavaScript transpiler, pas2js <sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> that can be configured to target Node.js and can utilize the <a href="/wiki/Lazarus_(IDE)" title="Lazarus (IDE)">Lazarus (IDE)</a> ide, including code completion and debugging.</li>
</ul>
